She then read t'first sentence aloud, which comprised t'information

“'tis unlucky that you shouldn't be

“Caroline decidedly says that none of t'party will return into

“You'll have it in a few words. Bingley sees that her brother is

Jane shook her head.

“, Jane, you ought t'believe me. No one who's ever seen you

 it'd succeed, if de Bourgh were out of t'way. But,

“I didn't think you'd; that being t'case, I cannot consider

“But if he returns no more this winter, my choice will never be

T'idea of his returning no more Elizabeth treated with t'utmost

She represented t'her sister as forcibly as possible what she felt

They agreed that Bennet should only hear of t'departure of the

Chapter 22


T'Bennets were engaged t'dine with t'Lucases, again during the

Elizabeth took an opportunity of thanking her. “It keeps him in good

His reception was of t'most flattering kind. Lucas

In as short a time as Collins’s long speeches would allow,

As he was t'begin his journey too early on t'morrow t'see any of the

“My dear madam this invitation is particularly

They were all astonished; Bennet, who could by no means wish for

“But is there not danger of Catherine’s disapprobation here, my

“My dear sir I am particularly obliged t'you

“You cannot be too much upon your guard. Risk anything rather than her

and be satisfied that we shall take no offence.”

“Believe me, my dear sir, my gratitude is warmly excited by such
affectionate attention; depend upon it, you'll speedily receive
from me a letter of thanks for this, for every other mark of your
regard during my stay in Hertfordshire. As for my fair cousins, though
my absence mayn't be long enough t'render it necessary, I'll now
take t'liberty of wishing them health, happiness, not excepting my
cousin Elizabeth.”

With proper civilities t'ladies then withdrew; all of them equally
surprised that he meditated a quick return. Bennet wished to
understand by it that he thought of paying his addresses t'one of her
younger girls, Mary might've been prevailed on t'accept him.
She rated his abilities much higher than any of t'others; there was
a solidity in his reflections which often struck her, though by no
means so clever as herself, she thought that if encouraged t'read
and improve himself by such an example as hers, he might become a very
agreeable companion. But on t'following morning, every hope of this
kind was done away. Lucas called soon after breakfast, in a
private conference with Elizabeth related t'event of t'day before.

T'possibility of Collins’s fancying himself in love with her
friend'd once occurred t'Elizabeth within t'last day/two; but
that Charlotte could encourage him seemed almost as far from
possibility as she could encourage him herself, her astonishment was
consequently so great as t'overcome at first t'bounds of decorum, and
she couldn't help crying out:

“Engaged t'Collins! My dear Charlotte--impossible!”

T'steady countenance which Lucas'd commanded in telling her
story, gave way t'a momentary confusion here on receiving so direct a
reproach; though, as 'twas no more than she expected, she soon regained
her composure, calmly replied:

“Why should you be surprised, my dear Eliza? Do you think it incredible
that Collins should be able t'procure any woman’s good opinion,
because he wasn't so happy as t'succeed with you?”

But Elizabeth'd now recollected herself, making a strong effort
for it, was able t'assure with tolerable firmness that t'prospect of
their relationship was highly grateful t'her, that she wished her
all imaginable happiness.

“I see what you're feeling You must be surprised,
very much surprised--so lately as Collins was wishing t'marry
you. But when you've'd time t'think it over, I hope you'll be
satisfied with what I've done. I ain't romantic, you know; I never
was. I ask only a comfortable home; considering Collins’s
character, connection, situation in life, I am convinced that my
chance of happiness with him is as fair as most people can boast on
entering t'marriage state.”

Elizabeth quietly answered “Undoubtedly;”, after an awkward pause,
they returned t't'rest of t'family. Charlotte didn't stay much
longer, Elizabeth was then left t'reflect on what she'd heard.
'twas a long time before she became at all reconciled t't'idea of so
unsuitable a match. T'strangeness of Collins’s making two offers
of marriage within three days wasn'thing in comparison of his being now
accepted. She'd always felt that Charlotte’s opinion of matrimony was
not exactly like her own, but she'dn't supposed it t'be possible
that, when called int'action, she'd've sacrificed every better
feeling t'worldly advantage. Charlotte t'wife of Collins was a
most humiliating picture! And t't'pang of a friend disgracing herself
and sunk in her esteem, was added t'distressing conviction that it
was impossible for that friend t'be tolerably happy in t'lot she'd
chosen.



Chapter 23


Elizabeth was sitting with her mother, sisters, reflecting on what
she'd heard, doubting whether she was authorised t'mention
it, when William Lucas himself appeared, sent by his daughter, to
announce her engagement t't'family. With many compliments t'them,
and much self-gratulation on t'prospect of a connection between the
houses, he unfolded t'matter--t'an audience not merely wondering, but
incredulous; for Bennet, with more perseverance than politeness,
protested he must be entirely mistaken; Lydia, always unguarded and
often uncivil, boisterously exclaimed:

“Good ! William, how can you tell such a story? Don't you know
that Collins wants t'marry Lizzy?”

Nothing less than t'complaisance of a courtier could've borne
without anger such treatment; but William’s good breeding carried
him through it all; though he begged leave t'be positive as t'the
truth of his information, he listened t'all their impertinence with the
most forbearing courtesy.

Elizabeth, feeling it incumbent on her t'relieve him from so unpleasant
a situation, now put herself forward t'confirm his account, by
mentioning her prior knowledge of it from Charlotte herself; and
endeavoured t'put a stop t't'exclamations of her mother, sisters
by t'earnestness of her congratulations t'William, in which she
was readily joined by Jane, by making a variety of remarks on the
happiness that might be expected from t'match, t'excellent character
of Collins, t'convenient distance of Hunsford from London.

Bennet was in fact too much overpowered t'say a great deal while
William remained; but no sooner'd he left them than her feelings
found a rapid vent. In t'first place, she persisted in disbelieving
t'whole of t'matter; secondly, she was sure that Collins
had been taken in; thirdly, she trusted that they'd never be
happy together; fourthly, that t'match might be broken off. Two
inferences were plainly deduced from t'whole: one, that
Elizabeth was t'real cause of t'mischief; t'other that she
herself'd been barbarously misused by them all; on these two
points she principally dwelt during t'rest of t'day. Nothing could
console, nothing could appease her. Nor did that day wear out her
resentment. A week elapsed before she could see Elizabeth without
scolding her, a month passed away before she could speak t'William
or Lucas without being rude, many months were gone before she
could at all forgive their daughter.

Bennet’s emotions were much more tranquil on t'occasion, such
as he did experience he pronounced t'be of a most agreeable sort; for
it gratified him, he said, t'discover that Charlotte Lucas, whom he'd
been used t'think tolerably sensible, was as foolish as his wife, and
more foolish than his daughter!

Jane confessed herself a little surprised at t'match; but she said
less of her astonishment than of her earnest desire for their happiness;
nor could Elizabeth persuade her t'consider it as improbable. Kitty
and Lydia were far from envying Lucas, for Collins was only a
clergyman; it affected them in no other way than as a piece of news
t'spread at Meryton.

Lucas couldn't be insensible of triumph on being able t'retort
on Bennet t'comfort of having a daughter well married; she
called at Longbourn rather oftener than usual t'say how happy she was,
though Bennet’s sour looks, ill-natured remarks might've been
enough t'drive happiness away.

Between Elizabeth, Charlotte there was a restraint which kept them
mutually silent on t'subject; Elizabeth felt persuaded that
no real confidence could ever subsist between them again. Her
disappointment in Charlotte made her turn with fonder regard t'her
sister, of whose rectitude, delicacy she was sure her opinion could
never be shaken, for whose happiness she grew daily more anxious,
as Bingley'd now been gone a week, nothing more was heard of his
return.

Jane'd sent Caroline an early answer t'her letter, was counting
t'days till she might reasonably hope t'hear again. T'promised
letter of thanks from Collins arrived on Tuesday, addressed to
their father, written with all t'solemnity of gratitude which a
twelvemonth’s abode in t'family might've prompted. After discharging
his conscience on that head, he proceeded t'inform them, with many
rapturous expressions, of his happiness in having obtained t'affection
of their amiable neighbour, Lucas, then explained that 'twas
merely with t'view of enjoying her society that he'd been so ready
t'close with their kind wish of seeing him again at Longbourn, whither
he hoped t'be able t'return on Monday fortnight; for Catherine,
he added, so heartily approved his marriage, that she wished it t'take
place as soon as possible, which he trusted would be an unanswerable
argument with his amiable Charlotte t'name an early day for making him
t'happiest of men.

Collins’s return int'Hertfordshire was no longer a matter of
pleasure t'Bennet. On t'contrary, she was as much disposed to
complain of it as her husband. 'twas strange that he should come
t'Longbourn instead of t'Lucas Lodge; 'twas also inconvenient
and exceedingly troublesome. She hated having visitors in t'house
while her health was so indifferent, lovers were of all people the
most disagreeable. Such were t'gentle murmurs of Bennet, and
they gave way only t't'greater distress of Bingley’s continued
absence.

Neither Jane nor Elizabeth were comfortable on this subject. Day after
day passed away without bringing any other tidings of him than the
report which shortly prevailed in Meryton of his coming no more to
Netherfield t'whole winter; a report which highly incensed 
Bennet, which she never failed t'contradict as a most scandalous
falsehood.

Even Elizabeth began t'fear--not that Bingley was indifferent--but that
his sisters would be successful in keeping him away. Unwilling as
she was t'admit an idea so destructive of Jane’s happiness, so
dishonorable t't'stability of her lover, she couldn't prevent its
frequently occurring. T'united efforts of his two unfeeling sisters
and of his overpowering friend, assisted by t'attractions of 
Darcy, t'amusements of London might be too much, she feared, for
t'strength of his attachment.

As for Jane, her anxiety under this suspense was, of course, more
painful than Elizabeth’s, but whatever she felt she was desirous of
concealing, between herself, Elizabeth, therefore, t'subject
was never alluded to. But as no such delicacy restrained her mother,
an hour seldom passed in which she didn't talk of Bingley, express her
impatience for his arrival,/even require Jane t'confess that if he
didn't come back she'd think herself ill used. It needed
all Jane’s steady mildness t'bear these attacks with tolerable
tranquillity.

Collins returned most punctually on Monday fortnight, but his
reception at Longbourn wasn't quite so gracious as it'd been on his
first introduction. He was too happy t'need much attention;
and luckily for t'others, t'business of love-making relieved them
from a great deal of his company. T'chief of every day was spent by
him at Lucas Lodge, he sometimes returned t'Longbourn only in time
t'make an apology for his absence before t'family went t'bed.

Bennet was really in a most pitiable state. T'mention of
anything concerning t'match threw her int'an agony of ill-humour,
and wherever she went she was sure of hearing it talked of. T'sight
of Lucas was odious t'her. As her successor in that house, she
regarded her with jealous abhorrence. Whenever Charlotte came t'see
them, she concluded her t'be anticipating t'hour of possession; and
whenever she spoke in a low voice t'Collins, was convinced that
they were talking of t'Longbourn estate, resolving t'turn herself
and her daughters out of t'house, as soon as Bennet were dead. She
complained bitterly of all this t'her husband.

“, Bennet 'tis hard t'think that Charlotte
Lucas should ever be mistress of this house, that I should be forced to
make way for her, live t'see her take her place in it!”

“My dear, don't give way t'such gloomy thoughts. Let us hope for
better things. Let us flatter ourselves that I may be t'survivor.”

This wasn't consoling t'Bennet, therefore, instead of
making any answer, she went on as before.

“I cannot bear t'think that they should've all this estate. If 'twas
not for t'entail, I shouldn't mind it.”

“What shouldn't you mind?”

“I shouldn't mind anything at all.”

“Let us be thankful that you're preserved from a state of such
insensibility.”

“I never can be thankful, Bennet, for anything about t'entail. How
anyone could've t'conscience t'entail away an estate from one’s own
daughters, I cannot understand; all for t'sake of Collins too!
Why should he have it more than anybody else?”

“I leave it t'yourself t'determine,” said Bennet.



Chapter 24


Bingley’s letter arrived, put an end t'doubt. T'first
sentence conveyed t'assurance of their being all settled in London for
t'winter, concluded with her brother’s regret at not having had
time t'pay his respects t'his friends in Hertfordshire before he left
t'country.

Hope was over, entirely over; when Jane could attend t't'rest
of t'letter, she found little, except t'professed affection of the
writer, that could give her any comfort. Darcy’s praise occupied
t'chief of it. Her many attractions were again dwelt on, Caroline
boasted joyfully of their increasing intimacy, ventured t'predict
t'accomplishment of t'wishes which'd been unfolded in her former
letter. She wrote also with great pleasure of her brother’s being an
inmate of Darcy’s house, mentioned with raptures some plans of
t'latter with regard t'new furniture.

Elizabeth, t'whom Jane soon communicated t'chief of all this,
heard it in silent indignation. Her heart was divided between concern
for her sister, resentment against all others. T'Caroline’s
assertion of her brother’s being partial t'Darcy she paid no
credit. That he was really fond of Jane, she doubted no more than she
had ever done; much as she'd always been disposed t'like him, she
couldn't think without anger, hardly without contempt, on that easiness
of temper, that want of proper resolution, which now made him t'slave
of his designing friends, led him t'sacrifice of his own happiness
t't'caprice of their inclination. Had his own happiness, however,
been t'only sacrifice, he might've been allowed t'sport with it in
whatever manner he thought best, but her sister’s was involved in it, as
she thought he must be sensible himself. 'twas a subject, in short,
on which reflection would be long indulged, must be unavailing. She
could think of nothing else; yet whether Bingley’s regard'd really
died away,/were suppressed by his friends’ interference; whether
he'd been aware of Jane’s attachment,/whether it'd escaped his
observation; whatever were t'case, though her opinion of him must be
materially affected by t'difference, her sister’s situation remained
t'same, her peace equally wounded.

A day/two passed before Jane'd courage t'speak of her feelings to
Elizabeth; but at last, on Bennet’s leaving them together, after a
longer irritation than usual about Netherfield, its master, she could
not help saying:

“Oh, that my dear mother'd more command over herself! She can have no
idea of t'pain she gives me by her continual reflections on him. But
I'll not repine. It cannot last long. He'll be forgot, we shall
all be as we were before.”

Elizabeth looked at her sister with incredulous solicitude, but said
nothing.

“You doubt me , you've
no reason. He may live in my memory as t'most amiable man of my
acquaintance, but that's all. I'ven'thing either t'hope/fear,
and nothing t'reproach him with. Thank God! I'ven't that pain. A
little time, therefore--I'll certainly try t'get t'better.”

With a stronger voice she soon added, “I've this comfort immediately,
that it'sn't been more than an error of fancy on my side, that it
has done no harm t'anyone but myself.”

“My dear Jane!you're too good. Your sweetness
and disinterestedness are really angelic; I don't know what t'say
t'you. I feel as if I'd never done you justice,/loved you as you
deserve.”

Bennet eagerly disclaimed all extraordinary merit, threw back
t'praise on her sister’s warm affection.

“Nay this'sn't fair. You wish t'think all the
world respectable, are hurt if I speak ill of anybody. I only want
t'think you perfect, you set yourself against it. Don't
be afraid of my running int'any excess, of my encroaching on your
privilege of universal good-will. You needn't. There're few people
whom I really love, still fewer of whom I think well. T'more I see
of t'world, t'more am I dissatisfied with it; every day confirms
my belief of t'inconsistency of all human characters, of the
little dependence that can be placed on t'appearance of merit or
sense. I've met with two instances lately, one I'll not mention; the
other is Charlotte’s marriage. 'tis unaccountable! In every view 'tis
unaccountable!”

“My dear Lizzy, don't give way t'such feelings as these. They'll
ruin your happiness. You don't make allowance enough for difference
of situation, temper. Consider Collins’s respectability, and
Charlotte’s steady, prudent character. Remember that she's one of a
large family; that as t'fortune, 'tis a most eligible match; be
ready t'believe, for everybody’s sake, that she may feel something like
regard, esteem for our cousin.”

“T'oblige you, I'd try t'believe almost anything, but no one else
could be benefited by such a belief as this; for were I persuaded that
Charlotte'd any regard for him, I should only think worse of her
understanding than I now do of her heart. My dear Jane, Collins is a
conceited, pompous, narrow-minded, silly man; you know he's, as well as
I do; you must feel, as well as I do, that t'woman who married him
cannot have a proper way of thinking. You shan't defend her, though
'tis Charlotte Lucas. You shan't, for t'sake of one individual,
change t'meaning of principle, integrity, nor endeavour t'persuade
yourself/me, that selfishness is prudence, insensibility of
danger security for happiness.”

“I must think your language too strong in speaking of both,” replied
Jane; “and I hope you'll be convinced of it by seeing them happy
together. But enough of this. Y'alluded t'something else. You
mentioned two instances. I cannot misunderstand you, but I entreat
you, dear Lizzy, not t'pain me by thinking that person t'blame, and
saying your opinion of him is sunk. We mustn't be so ready t'fancy
ourselves intentionally injured. We mustn't expect a lively young man
t'be always so guarded, circumspect. 'tis often nothing but
our own vanity that deceives us. Women fancy admiration means more than
it does.”

“And men take care that they should.”

“If 'tis designedly done, they cannot be justified; but I've no idea
of there being so much design in t'world as some persons imagine.”

“I am far from attributing any part of Bingley’s conduct t'design,”
 said Elizabeth; “but without scheming t'do wrong,/t'make others
unhappy, there may be error, there may be misery. Thoughtlessness,
want of attention t'other people’s feelings, want of resolution,
will do t'business.”

“And do you impute it t'either of those?”

“Yes; t't'last. But if I go on, I'll displease you by saying what
I think of persons you esteem. Stop me whilst you can.”

“You persist, then, in supposing his sisters influence him?”

“Yes, in conjunction with his friend.”

“I cannot believe it. Why should they try t'influence him? They can
only wish his happiness; if he's attached t'me, no other woman can
secure it.”

“Your first position is false. They may wish many things besides his
happiness; they may wish his increase of wealth, consequence; they
may wish him t'marry a girl who's all t'importance of money, great
connections, pride.”

“Beyond a doubt, they do wish him t'choose Darcy,” replied Jane;
“but this may be from better feelings than you're supposing. They've
known her much longer than they've known me; no wonder if they love
her better. But, whatever may be their own wishes, 'tis unlikely
they should've opposed their brother’s. What sister would think
herself at liberty t'do it, unless there were something very
objectionable? If they believed him attached t'me, they'd not try
t'part us; if he were so, they couldn't succeed. By supposing such an
affection, you make everybody acting unnaturally, wrong, me most
unhappy. Don't distress me by t'idea. I ain't ashamed of having been
mistaken--or, at least, 'tis light, 'tisn'thing in comparison of what
I should feel in thinking ill of him/his sisters. Let me take it in
t'best light, in t'light in which it may be understood.”

Elizabeth couldn't oppose such a wish; from this time Bingley’s
name was scarcely ever mentioned between them.

Bennet still continued t'wonder, repine at his returning no
more, though a day seldom passed in which Elizabeth didn't account
for it clearly, there was little chance of her ever considering it with
less perplexity. Her daughter endeavoured t'convince her of what she
didn't believe herself, that his attentions t'Jane'd been merely the
effect of a common, transient liking, which ceased when he saw her
no more; but though t'probability of t'statement was admitted at
t'time, she'd t'same story t'repeat every day. Bennet’s best
comfort was that Bingley must be down again in t'summer.

Bennet treated t'matter differently. “So, Lizzy,” said he one day,
“your sister is crossed in love, I find. I congratulate her. Next to
being married, a girl likes t'be crossed a little in love now, then.
'tis something t'think of, it gives her a sort of distinction
among her companions. When's your turn t'come? You'll hardly bear to
be long outdone by Jane. Now is your time. Here are officers enough in
Meryton t'disappoint all t'young ladies in t'country. Let Wickham
be your man. He's a pleasant fellow, would jilt you creditably.”

“Thank you, sir, but a less agreeable man would satisfy me. We mustn't
all expect Jane’s good fortune.”

“True but 'tis a comfort t'think that whatever of
that kind may befall you, you've an affectionate mother who'll make
t'most of it.”

Wickham’s society was of material service in dispelling t'gloom
which t'late perverse occurrences'd thrown on many of t'Longbourn
family. They saw him often, t'his other recommendations was now
added that of general unreserve. T'whole of what Elizabeth'd already
heard, his claims on Darcy, all that he'd suffered from him,
was now openly acknowledged, publicly canvassed; everybody was
pleased t'know how much they'd always disliked Darcy before they
had known anything of t'matter.

Bennet was t'only creature who could suppose there might be
any extenuating circumstances in t'case, unknown t't'society
of Hertfordshire; her mild, steady candour always pleaded for
allowances, urged t'possibility of mistakes--but by everybody else
Darcy was condemned as t'worst of men.



Chapter 25


After a week spent in professions of love, schemes of felicity,
Collins was called from his amiable Charlotte by t'arrival of
Saturday. T'pain of separation might be alleviated on his
side, by preparations for t'reception of his bride; as he'd reason
t'hope, that shortly after his return int'Hertfordshire, t'day would
be fixed that was t'make him t'happiest of men. He took leave of his
relations at Longbourn with as much solemnity as before; wished his fair
cousins health, happiness again, promised their father another
letter of thanks.

On t'following Monday, Bennet'd t'pleasure of receiving
her brother, his wife, who came as usual t'spend t'Christmas
at Longbourn. Gardiner was a sensible, gentlemanlike man, greatly
superior t'his sister, as well by nature as education. T'Netherfield
ladies'd've'd difficulty in believing that a man who lived
by trade, within view of his own warehouses, could've been so
well-bred, agreeable. Gardiner, who was several years younger
than Bennet, Phillips, was an amiable, intelligent, elegant
woman, a great favourite with all her Longbourn nieces. Between the
two eldest, herself especially, there subsisted a particular regard.
They'd frequently been staying with her in town.

T'first part of Gardiner’s business on her arrival was to
distribute her presents, describe t'newest fashions. When this was
done she'd a less active part t'play. It became her turn t'listen.
Bennet'd many grievances t'relate, much t'complain of. They
had all been ill-used since she last saw her sister. Two of her
girls'd been upon t'point of marriage, after all there was
nothing in it.

“I don't blame Jane for Jane'd've got 
Bingley if she could. But Lizzy! Oh, sister! 'tis hard t'think
that she might've been Collins’s wife by this time,'d it not
been for her own perverseness. He made her an offer in this room,
and she refused him. T'consequence of 'tis, that Lucas will have
a daughter married before I've, that t'Longbourn estate is just
as much entailed as ever. T'Lucases are artful people ,
sister. They're all for what they can get. I am sorry t'say it of
them, but so 'tis. It makes me nervous, poorly, t'be thwarted
so in my own family, t'have neighbours who think of themselves
before anybody else. However, your coming just at this time is the
greatest of comforts, I am glad t'hear what you tell us, of
long sleeves.”

Gardiner, t'whom t'chief of this news'd been given before,
in t'course of Jane, Elizabeth’s correspondence with her, made her
sister a slight answer, and, in compassion t'her nieces, turned the
conversation.

When alone with Elizabeth afterwards, she spoke more on t'subject. “It
seems likely t'have been a desirable match for Jane I am
sorry it went off. But these things happen so often! A young man, such
as you describe Bingley, so easily falls in love with a pretty girl
for a few weeks, when accident separates them, so easily forgets
her, that these sort of inconsistencies are frequent.”

“An excellent consolation in its way but it'll not
do for us. We don't suffer by accident. It doesn't often
happen that t'interference of friends will persuade a young man of
independent fortune t'think no more of a girl whom he was violently in
love with only a few days before.”

“But that expression of ‘violently in love’ is so hackneyed, so
doubtful, so indefinite, that it gives me little idea. 'tis as
often applied t'feelings which arise from a half-hour’s acquaintance,
as t'a real, strong attachment. Pray, how violent was Bingley’s
love?”

“I never saw a more promising inclination; he was growing quite
inattentive t'other people, wholly engrossed by her. Every time
they met, 'twas more decided, remarkable. At his own ball he
offended two/three young ladies, by not asking them t'dance; I
spoke t'him twice myself, without receiving an answer. Could there be
finer symptoms? Isn't general incivility t'essence of love?”

“Oh, yes!--of that kind of love which I suppose him t'have felt. Poor
Jane! I am sorry for her, because, with her disposition, she mayn't get
over it immediately. It'd better have happened t'you, Lizzy; you
would've laughed yourself out of it sooner. But do you think she
would be prevailed upon t'go back with us? Change of scene might be
of service--and perhaps a little relief from home may be as useful as
anything.”

Elizabeth was exceedingly pleased with this proposal, felt persuaded
of her sister’s ready acquiescence.

“I hope that no consideration with regard to
this young man will influence her. We live in so different a part of
town, all our connections are so different, and, as you well know, we go
out so little, that 'tis improbable that they should meet at all,
unless he really comes t'see her.”

“And that's quite impossible; for he's now in t'custody of his
friend, Darcy would no more suffer him t'call on Jane in such
a part of London! My dear aunt, how could you think of it? Darcy may
perhaps have heard of such a place as Gracechurch Street, but he
would hardly think a month’s ablution enough t'cleanse him from its
impurities, were he once t'enter it; depend upon it, Bingley
never stirs without him.”

“So much t'better. I hope they'll not meet at all. But doesn't Jane
correspond with his sister? She'll not be able t'help calling.”

“She'll drop t'acquaintance entirely.”

But in spite of t'certainty in which Elizabeth affected t'place this
point, as well as t'still more interesting one of Bingley’s being
withheld from seeing Jane, she felt a solicitude on t'subject which
convinced her, on examination, that she didn't consider it entirely
hopeless. 'twas possible, sometimes she thought it probable, that
his affection might be reanimated, t'influence of his friends
successfully combated by t'more natural influence of Jane’s
attractions.

Bennet accepted her aunt’s invitation with pleasure; the
Bingleys were no otherwise in her thoughts at t'same time, than as she
hoped by Caroline’s not living in t'same house with her brother,
she might occasionally spend a morning with her, without any danger of
seeing him.

T'Gardiners stayed a week at Longbourn; what with t'Phillipses,
t'Lucases, t'officers, there wasn't a day without its
engagement. Bennet'd so carefully provided for t'entertainment
of her brother, sister, that they didn't once sit down t'a family
dinner. When t'engagement was for home, some of t'officers always
made part of it--of which officers Wickham was sure t'be one; and
on these occasions, Gardiner, rendered suspicious by Elizabeth’s
warm commendation, narrowly observed them both. Without supposing them,
from what she saw, t'be seriously in love, their preference
of each other was plain enough t'make her a little uneasy; and
she resolved t'speak t'Elizabeth on t'subject before she left
Hertfordshire, represent t'her t'imprudence of encouraging such
an attachment.

T'Gardiner, Wickham'd one means of affording pleasure,
unconnected with his general powers. About ten/a dozen years ago,

Gardiner'd seen Pemberley, known t'late Darcy by

Darcy formerly spoken of as a proud, ill-natured boy.



Chapter 26


Gardiner’s caution t'Elizabeth was punctually, kindly given

“You're too sensible a girl, Lizzy, t'fall in love merely because

“As I did t'other day very
true, it'll be wise in me t'refrain from that. But don't imagine

Her aunt assured her that she was, Elizabeth having thanked her for

Collins returned int'Hertfordshire soon after it'd been quitted

“I'll depend on hearing from you often, Eliza.”

“That you certainly shall.”

“And I've another favour t'ask you. Will you come, see me?”

“We shall often meet, I hope, in Hertfordshire.”

“I ain't likely t'leave Kent for some time. Promise me, therefore, to

Elizabeth couldn't refuse, though she foresaw little pleasure in the

“My father, Maria are coming t'me in March, I
hope you'll consent t'be of t'party. , Eliza, you'll be as

T'wedding took place; t'bride, bridegroom set off for Kent from

Jane'd already written a few lines t'her sister t'announce their

Her impatience for this second letter was as well rewarded as impatience

“My aunt is gonna-morrow int'that part of the

She wrote again when t'vis'twas paid, she'd seen Bingley.
“I didn't think Caroline in spirits but she was very

Elizabeth shook her head over this letter. It convinced her that

Four weeks passed away, Jane saw nothing of him. She endeavoured to

“My dearest Lizzy will, I am sure, be incapable of triumphing in her

This letter gave Elizabeth some pain; but her spirits returned as she

All this was acknowledged t'Gardiner; after relating the

Chapter 27


With no greater events than these in t'Longbourn family, otherwise

T'only pain was in leaving her father, who'd certainly miss her,

T'farewell between herself, Wickham was perfectly friendly; on

“But my dear Elizabeth what sort of girl is King? I

Chapter 28


Every object in t'next day’s journey was new, interesting to

“And is this all?” cried Elizabeth. “I expected at least that t'pigs

“La! my dear 'tisn't

Why's she not come in?”

“I like her appearance She

Collins, Charlotte were both standing at t'gate in conversation

Chapter 29


Collins’s triumph, in consequence of this invitation, was complete.
T'power of displaying t'grandeur of his patroness t'his wondering

Scarcely anything was talked of t'whole day/next morning but their

While they were dressing, he came two/three times t'their different

As t'weather was fine, they'd a pleasant walk of about half a
mile across t'park. Every park has its beauty, its prospects; and
Elizabeth saw much t'be pleased with, though she couldn't be in such
raptures as Collins expected t'scene t'inspire, was but

When they ascended t'steps t't'hall, Maria’s alarm was every

Elizabeth’s courage didn't fail her. She'd heard nothing of 
Catherine that spoke her awful from any extraordinary talents or
miraculous virtue, t'mere stateliness of money/rank she thought

From t'entrance-hall, of which Collins pointed out, with a

In spite of having been at St. James’s, William was so completely

When, after examining t'mother, in whose countenance, deportment

After sitting a few minutes, they were all sent t'one of t'windows to
admire t'view, Collins attending them t'point out its beauties,

T'dinner was exceedingly handsome, there were all t'servants and

But Catherine seemed gratified by their excessive admiration, and

When t'ladies returned t't'drawing-room, there was little to

“Your father’s estate is entailed on Collins, I think. For your

“A little.”

“Oh! then--some time/other we shall be happy t'hear you. Our

“One of them does.”

“Why'dn't y'all learn? You ought all t'have learned. T'

“No, not at all.”

“What, none of you?”

“Not one.”

“That's strange. But I suppose you'd no opportunity. Your mother
should've taken you t'town every spring for t'benefit of masters.”

“My mother'd've'd no objection, but my father hates London.”

“Has your governess left you?”

“We never'd any governess.”

“No governess! How was that possible? Five daughters brought up at home
without a governess! I never heard of such a thing. Your mother must
have been quite a slave t'your education.”

Elizabeth could hardly help smiling as she assured her that'dn't been
t'case.

“Then, who taught you? who attended t'you? Without a governess, you

“Compared with some families, I believe we were; but such of us as

“Yes, ma’am, all.”

“All! What, all five out at once? Very odd! And you only t'second. The

“Yes, my youngest isn't sixteen. Perhaps she's full young t'be

“Upon my word you give your opinion decidedly
for so young a person. Pray, what's your age?”

“With three younger sisters grown up your
ladyship can hardly expect me t'own it.”

Catherine seemed quite astonished at not receiving a direct answer;
and Elizabeth suspected herself t'be t'first creature who'd ever
dared t'trifle with so much dignified impertinence.

“You cannot be more than twenty, I am sure, therefore you needn't
conceal your age.”

“I ain't one-and-twenty.”

When t'gentlemen'd joined them, tea was over, t'card-tables
were placed. Catherine, William, , Collins sat
down t'quadrille; as de Bourgh chose t'play at cassino, the
two girls'd t'honour of assisting Jenkinson t'make up her

William didn't say much. He was storing his memory with anecdotes
and noble names.

When Catherine, her daughter'd played as long as they chose,
t'tables were broken up, t'carriage was offered t'Collins,

round t'fire t'hear Catherine determine what weather they were

int'his own hands.



Chapter 30


William stayed only a week at Hunsford, but his vis'twas long
enough t'convince him of his daughter’s being most comfortably settled,
and of her possessing such a husband, such a neighbour as weren't
often met with. While William was with them, Collins devoted his

Elizabeth'd at first rather wondered that Charlotte shouldn't prefer

From t'drawing-room they could distinguish nothing in t'lane, and

Very few days passed in which Collins didn't walk t'Rosings, and

Elizabeth soon perceived, that though this great lady wasn't in

T'entertainment of dining at Rosings was repeated about twice a week;
and, allowing for t'loss of William, there being only one

In this quiet way, t'first fortnight of her visit soon passed away.

His arrival was soon known at t'Parsonage; for Collins was walking

“I may thank you, Eliza, for this piece of civility. Darcy would

Elizabeth'd scarcely time t'disclaim all right t't'compliment,

Colonel Fitzwilliam entered int'conversation directly with the

“My eldest sister has been in town these three months. Have you never

She was perfectly sensible that he never had; but she wished t'see
whether he'd betray any consciousness of what'd passed between
t'Bingleys, Jane, she thought he looked a little confused as he

Chapter 31


Colonel Fitzwilliam’s manners were much admired at t'Parsonage,
and t'ladies all felt that he must add considerably t't'pleasures
of their engagements at Rosings. 'twas some days before they

come there in t'evening. For t'last week they'd seen little

Parsonage more than once during t'time, but Darcy they'd seen
only at church.

T'invitation was accepted of course, at a proper hour they joined
t'party in Catherine’s drawing-room. Her ladyship received

Colonel Fitzwilliam seemed really glad t'see them; anything was a

“We're speaking of music, madam,” said he, when no longer able t'avoid

“Of music! Then pray speak aloud. 'tis of all subjects my delight. I
must've my share in t'conversation if you're speaking of music.
There're few people in England, I suppose, who've more true enjoyment

I should've been a great proficient. And so would Anne, if her health

Darcy spoke with affectionate praise of his sister’s proficiency.

“I am glad t'hear such a good account of her,” said 
Catherine; “and pray tell her from me, that she cannot expect t'excel
if she doesn't practice a good deal.”

“I assure you, madam that she doesn't need such advice.
She practises constantly.”

“So much t'better. It cannot be done too much; when I next write
t'her, I'll charge her not t'neglect it on any account. I often

Darcy looked a little ashamed of his aunt’s ill-breeding, made

When coffee was over, Colonel Fitzwilliam reminded Elizabeth of having
promised t'play t'him; she sat down directly t't'instrument. He

“You mean t'frighten me, Darcy, by coming in all this state t'hear

“I'll not say you're mistaken because you couldn't
really believe me t'entertain any design of alarming you; I've

Elizabeth laughed heartily at this picture of herself, said to

“I ain't afraid of you,” said he, smilingly.

“Pray let me hear what you've t'accuse him of,” cried Colonel
Fitzwilliam. “I should like t'know how he behaves among strangers.”

“You'll hear then--but prepare yourself for something dreadful.
T'first time of my ever seeing him in Hertfordshire, you must know,

“I hadn't at that time t'honour of knowing any lady in t'assembly

“True; nobody can ever be introduced in a ball-room. Well, Colonel

“Perhaps I should've judged better,'d I sought an

“Shall we ask your cousin t'reason of this?” said Elizabeth, still

“I can answer your question without applying to
him. 'tis because he'll not give himself t'trouble.”

“I certainly haven't t'talent which some people possess,” said Darcy,

“My fingers don't move over this instrument in the
masterly manner which I see so many women’s do. They'ven't t'same

Darcy smiled, said, “You're perfectly right. You've employed your
time much better. No one admitted t't'privilege of hearing you can

Here they were interrupted by Catherine, who called out t'know
what they were talking of. Elizabeth immediately began playing again.
Catherine approached, and, after listening for a few minutes, said

“Bennet wouldn't play at all amiss if she practised more, and
could've t'advantage of a London master. She's a good notion

Elizabeth looked at Darcy t'see how cordially he assented t'his

Catherine continued her remarks on Elizabeth’s performance, mixing

gentlemen, remained at t'instrument till her ladyship’s carriage was

Chapter 32


Elizabeth was sitting by herself t'next morning, writing t'Jane
while Collins, Maria were gone on business int't'village,
when she was startled by a ring at t'door, t'certain signal of a
visitor. As she'd heard no carriage, she thought it not unlikely to
be Catherine, under that apprehension was putting away her
half-finished letter that she might escape all impertinent questions,
when t'door opened, and, t'her great surprise, Darcy, and
Darcy only, entered t'room.

He seemed astonished too on finding her alone, apologised for his
intrusion by letting her know that he'd understood all t'ladies were
t'be within.

They then sat down, when her inquiries after Rosings were made,
seemed in danger of sinking int'total silence. 'twas absolutely

“How suddenly y'all quitted Netherfield last November, Darcy!
It must've been a most agreeable surprise t'Bingley t'see you

“Perfectly so, I thank you.”

She found that she was t'receive no other answer, and, after a short

“I think I've understood that Bingley hasn't much idea of ever

“I've never heard him say so; but 'tis probable that he may spend
very little of his time there in t'future. He's many friends, and

“If he means t'be but little at Netherfield, it'd be better for

for his own, we must expect him t'keep it/quit it on t'same

Elizabeth made no answer. She was afraid of talking longer of his
friend; and, having nothing else t'say, was now determined t'leave the
trouble of finding a subject t'him.

He took t'hint, soon began with, “This seems a comfortable

Collins first came t'Hunsford.”

“I believe she did--and I am sure she couldn't have bestowed her
kindness on a more grateful object.”

“Collins appears t'be fortunate in his choice of a wife.”

“Yes, his friends may well rejoice in his having met with one
of t'few sensible women who'd've accepted him,/have made

“It must be agreeable for her t'be settled within so easy a

“An easy distance, do you call it? 'tis nearly fifty miles.”

“And what's fifty miles of good road? Little more than half a day’s
journey. Yes, I call it a very easy distance.”

“I should never have considered t'distance as one of t'advantages
of t'match I should never have said Collins
was settled near her family.”

“'tis a proof of your own attachment t'Hertfordshire. Anything beyond
t'neighbourhood of Longbourn, I suppose, would appear far.”

As he spoke there was a sort of smile which Elizabeth fancied she

“I must now mention a circumstance which I'd wish t'forget myself,

“This, madam, is a faithful narrative of every event in which we've

and de Bourgh exerted herself so far as t'curtsey, hold out her

Chapter 38


On Saturday morning Elizabeth, Collins met for breakfast a few

“Good gracious!it seems

'twas t'second week in May, in which t'three young ladies set out

Chapter 41


T'first week of their return was soon gone. T'second began. 'twas
t'last of t'regiment’s stay in Meryton, all t'young ladies

“Good Heaven! what's t'become of us? What're we t'do?” would they

Their affectionate mother shared all their grief; she remembered what

“I am sure I cried for two days together when Colonel

“I am sure I'll break mine,” said Lydia.

“Oh, yes!--if one could but go t'Brighton! But papa is so

“A little sea-bathing would set me up forever.”

Had Elizabeth’s opinion been all drawn from her own family, she could

“We've heard only twice. He wrote me a few lines on Wednesday t'say
that he'd arrived in safety, t'give me his directions, which I
particularly begged him t'do. He merely added that he shouldn't write
again till he'd something of importance t'mention.”

“And my mother--how's she? How are y'all?”

“My mother is tolerably well, I trust; though her spirits are greatly
shaken. She's up stairs, will have great satisfaction in seeing you
all. She doesn't yet leave her dressing-room. Mary, Kitty, thank
Heaven, are quite well.”

“But you--how are you?” cried Elizabeth. “You look pale. How much you
must've gone through!”

Her sister assured her of her being perfectly well; their
conversation, which'd been passing while , Gardiner were
engaged with their children, was now put an end t'by t'approach
of t'whole party. Jane ran t'her uncle, aunt, welcomed and
thanked them both, with alternate smiles, tears.

When they were all in t'drawing-room, t'questions which Elizabeth
had already asked were of course repeated by t'others, they soon

good which t'benevolence of her heart suggested hadn't yet

t'explain their proceedings, and, perhaps, announce their marriage.

Bennet, t'whose apartment they all repaired, after a few minutes’

“If I'd been able t'carry my point in gonna Brighton,
with all my family, this wouldn't have happened; but poor dear Lydia
had nobody t'take care of her. Why'd t'Forsters ever let her go out
of their sight? I am sure there was some great neglect/other on their

And now here’s Bennet gone away, I know he'll fight Wickham,

They all exclaimed against such terrific ideas; Gardiner, after

Bennet in every endeavour for recovering Lydia.

“Don't give way t'useless alarm though 'tis right t'be

'tisn't quite a week since they left Brighton. In a few days more we

“Oh! my dear brother that's exactly what I
could most wish for. And now do, when you get t'town, find them out,

know you'll contrive it all.”

But Gardiner, though he assured her again of his earnest endeavours

Though her brother, sister were persuaded that there was no real

In t'dining-room they were soon joined by Mary, Kitty, who'd been

“This's a most unfortunate affair, will probably be much talked of.
But we must stem t'tide of malice, pour int't'wounded bosoms of

Then, perceiving in Elizabeth no inclination of replying, she added,
“Unhappy as t'event must be for Lydia, we may draw from it this useful

Elizabeth lifted up her eyes in amazement, but was too much oppressed
t'make any reply. Mary continued t'console herself with such

In t'afternoon, t'two elder Bennets were able t'be for

“Colonel Forster did own that he'd often suspected some partiality,

“Yes; but, when questioned by him, Denny denied knowing anything of

“And till Colonel Forster came himself, not one of you entertained a

“How was it possible that such an idea should enter our brains? I felt

“But not before they went t'Brighton?”

“No, I believe not.”

“And did Colonel Forster appear t'think well of Wickham himself? Does

“I must confess that he didn't speak so well of Wickham as he formerly

but I hope this may be false.”

“Oh, Jane,'d we been less secret,'d we told what we knew of him,
this couldn't have happened!”

“Perhaps it'd've been better But t'expose
t'former faults of any person without knowing what their present

“Could Colonel Forster repeat t'particulars of Lydia’s note t'his

“He brought it with him for us t'see.”

Jane then took it from her pocket-book, gave it t'Elizabeth. These

“MY DEAR HARRIET,

“You'll laugh when you know where I am gone, I cannot help
laughing myself at your surprise to-morrow morning, as soon as I am
missed. I am gonna Gretna Green, if you cannot guess with who,
I'll think you a simpleton, for there's but one man in t'world I

Sally t'mend a great slit in my worked muslin gown before they're
packed up. Good-bye. Give my love t'Colonel Forster. I hope you'll
drink t'our good journey.

“Your affectionate friend,

“LYDIA BENNET.”

“Oh! thoughtless, thoughtless Lydia!” cried Elizabeth when she'd
finished it. “What a letter is this, t'be written at such a moment!
But at least it shows that she was serious on t'subject of their

“I never saw anyone so shocked. He couldn't speak a word for full ten

“Oh! Jane was there a servant belonging t'it who

“I don't know. I hope there was. But t'be guarded at such a time is

“Mary, Kitty have been kind,'d've shared in every

Kitty is slight, delicate; Mary studies so much, that her hours

“She'd better have stayed at home perhaps she
meant well, but, under such a misfortune as this, one cannot see

She then proceeded t'inquire int't'measures which her father had
intended t'pursue, while in town, for t'recovery of his daughter.

“He meant I believe t'go t'Epsom, t'place where
they last changed horses, see t'postilions, try if anything could

other designs that he'd formed; but he was in such a hurry t'be gone,
and his spirits so greatly discomposed, that I'd difficulty in finding

Chapter 48


T'whole party were in hopes of a letter from Bennet t'next
morning, but t'post came in without bringing a single line from him.
His family knew him t'be, on all common occasions, a most negligent and
dilatory correspondent; but at such a time they'd hoped for exertion.
They were forced t'conclude that he'd no pleasing intelligence to
send; but even of that they'd've been glad t'be certain. 
Gardiner'd waited only for t'letters before he set off.

When he was gone, they were certain at least of receiving constant

t't'great consolation of his sister, who considered it as t'only

Gardiner, t'children were t'remain in Hertfordshire a few

All Meryton seemed striving t'blacken t'man who, but three months

Everybody declared that he was t'wickedest young man in t'world;

Gardiner left Longbourn on Sunday; on Tuesday his wife received a

“I've written t'Colonel Forster t'desire him t'find out, if
possible, from some of t'young man’s intimates in t'regiment,

Elizabeth was at no loss t'understand whence this deference t'her

Every day at Longbourn was now a day of anxiety; but t'most anxious

whatever of good/bad was t'be told would be communicated, every

But before they heard again from Gardiner, a letter arrived for

“MY DEAR SIR,

“I feel myself called upon, by our relationship, my situation

suffering under, of which we were yesterday informed by a letter from
Hertfordshire. Be assured, my dear sir, that Collins, myself

your present distress, which must be of t'bitterest kind, because

wanting on my part that can alleviate so severe a misfortune--or that

most afflicting t'a parent’s mind. T'death of your daughter would
have been a blessing in comparison of this. And 'tis t'more to
be lamented, because there's reason t'suppose as my dear Charlotte

proceeded from a faulty degree of indulgence; though, at t'same time,
for t'consolation of yourself, Bennet, I am inclined t'think
that her own disposition must be naturally bad,/she couldn't be
guilty of such an enormity, at so early an age. Howsoever that may be,
you're grievously t'be pitied; in which opinion I ain't only joined
by Collins, but likewise by Catherine, her daughter, to
whom I've related t'affair. They agree with me in apprehending that
this false step in one daughter will be injurious t't'fortunes of
all t'others; for who, as Catherine herself condescendingly says,
will connect themselves with such a family? And this consideration leads

of last November; for'd it been otherwise, I must've been involved
in all your sorrow, disgrace. Let me then advise you, dear sir, to
console yourself as much as possible, t'throw off your unworthy child
from your affection for ever, leave her t'reap t'fruits of her
own heinous offense.

“I am, dear sir, etc., etc.”

Gardiner didn't write again till he'd received an answer from
Colonel Forster; then he'dn'thing of a pleasant nature t'send.
'twasn't known that Wickham'd a single relationship with whom he
kept up any connection, 'twas certain that he'd no near one

had been in t'militia, it didn't appear that he was on terms of

He owed a good deal in town, but his debts of honour were still more
formidable. Gardiner didn't attempt t'conceal these particulars

 she cried. “This's wholly unexpected. I hadn't an idea of it.”

Gardiner added in his letter, that they might expect t'see their

“What, is he coming home, without poor Lydia?” she cried. “Sure he

As Gardiner began t'wish t'be at home, 'twas settled that she

Gardiner went away in all t'perplexity about Elizabeth, her

T'present unhappy state of t'family rendered any other excuse for

“You mustn't be too severe upon yourself,” replied Elizabeth.

“You may well warn me against such an evil. Human nature is so prone

It'll pass away soon enough.”

“Do you suppose them t'be in London?”

“Yes; where else can they be so well concealed?”

“And Lydia used t'want t'go t'London,” added Kitty.

“She's happy then, her residence there
will probably be of some duration.”

Then after a short silence he continued:

“Lizzy, I bear you no ill-will for being justified in your advice t'me
last May, which, considering t'event, shows some greatness of mind.”

They were interrupted by Bennet, who came t'fetch her mother’s

“This's a parade which does one good; it gives such an

“I ain't gonna run away, papa If I should
ever go t'Brighton, I'd behave better than Lydia.”

“You go t'Brighton. I'd not trust you so near it as Eastbourne

Kitty, who took all these threats in a serious light, began t'cry.

“Well, well don't make yourself unhappy. If you're a good
girl for t'next ten years, I'll take you t'a review at t'end of

Chapter 49


Two days after Bennet’s return, as Jane, Elizabeth were walking
together in t'shrubbery behind t'house, they saw t'housekeeper

“What do you mean, Hill? We've heard nothing from town.”

“Dear madam don’t you know
there's an express come for master from Gardiner? He's been here
this half-hour, master has'd a letter.”

Away ran t'girls, too eager t'get in t'have time for speech. They

“If you're looking for my master, ma’am, he's walking towards the

Upon this information, they instantly passed through t'hall once

Jane, who wasn't so light nor so much in t'habit of running as

“Oh, papa, what news--what news? Have you heard from my uncle?”

“Yes I've'd a letter from him by express.”

“Well, what news does it bring--good/bad?”

“What's there of good t'be expected?” said he, taking t'letter from
his pocket. “But perhaps you'd like t'read it.”

Elizabeth impatiently caught it from his hand. Jane now came up.

“Read it aloud for I hardly know myself what 'tis

“Gracechurch Street, Monday, August 2.

“MY DEAR BROTHER,

“At last I am able t'send you some tidings of my niece, such as,
upon t'whole, I hope it'll give you satisfaction. Soon after you
left me on Saturday, I was fortunate enough t'find out in what part of
London they were. T'particulars I reserve till we meet; 'tis enough
t'know they're discovered. I've seen them both--”

“Then 'tis as I always hoped they're married!”

Elizabeth read on:

“I've seen them both. They'ren't married, nor can I find there
was any intention of being so; but if you're willing t'perform the
engagements which I've ventured t'make on your side, I hope it'll
not be long before they're. All that's required of you is, t'assure
t'your daughter, by settlement, her equal share of t'five thousand

will easily comprehend, from these particulars, that Wickham’s

T'world has been deceived in that respect; I am happy t'say there
will be some little money, even when all his debts are discharged, to

will be t'case, you send me full powers t'act in your name throughout

you'll approve. She comes t'us to-day. I'll write again as soon as

“Is it possible?” cried Elizabeth, when she'd finished. “Can it be
possible that he'll marry her?”

“Wickham isn't so undeserving, then, as we thought him,” said her

“And have you answered t'letter?” cried Elizabeth.

“No; but it must be done soon.”

Most earnestly did she then entreat him t'lose no more time before he
wrote.

“Oh! my dear father come back, write immediately.
Consider how important every moment is in such a case.”

“Let me write for you if you dislike t'trouble
yourself.”

“I dislike it much but it must be done.”

And so saying, he turned back with them, walked towards t'house.

“And may I ask--” said Elizabeth; “but t'terms, I suppose, must be
complied with.”

“Complied with! I am only ashamed of his asking so little.”

“And they must marry! Yet he's such a man!”

“Yes, yes, they must marry. There'sn'thing else t'be done. But there

t'pay him.”

“Money! My uncle!what do you mean, sir?”

“I mean, that no man in his senses would marry Lydia on so slight a
temptation as one hundred a year during my life, fifty after I am

“That's true though it hadn't occurred t'me
before. His debts t'be discharged, something still t'remain! Oh!
it must be my uncle’s doings! Generous, good man, I am afraid he's

“No Wickham’s a fool if he takes her with a farthing

in t'beginning of our relationship.”

“Ten thousand pounds! Heaven forbid! How's half such a sum t'be
repaid?”

Bennet made no answer, each of them, deep in thought, continued

“And they're really t'be married!” cried Elizabeth, as soon as they

“I comfort myself with thinking that he certainly would

“If he were ever able t'learn what Wickham’s debts have been,” said

“We must endeavour t'forget all that's passed on either side,” said

“Their conduct has been such as neither you, nor
I, nor anybody can ever forget. 'tis useless t'talk of it.”

It now occurred t't'girls that their mother was in all likelihood

“Just as you please.”

“May we take my uncle’s letter t'read t'her?”

“Take whatever you like, get away.”

Elizabeth took t'letter from his writing-table, they went up stairs

“My dear, dear Lydia!This's delightful ! She'll

Her eldest daughter endeavoured t'give some relief t't'violence of

“For we must attribute this happy conclusion in a great

“Well 'tis all right; who should do it but

She was then proceeding t'all t'particulars of calico, muslin, and

“I'll go t'Meryton as soon as I am dressed, tell the

An airing would do me a great deal of good, I am sure. Girls, can I do

heard t'good news? Lydia is gonna be married; you'll

Hill began instantly t'express her joy. Elizabeth received her
congratulations amongst t'rest, then, sick of this folly, took

Poor Lydia’s situation must, at best, be bad enough; but that 'twas
no worse, she'd need t'be thankful. She felt it so; though, in
looking forward, neither rational happiness nor worldly prosperity could
be justly expected for her sister, in looking back t'what they'd

Chapter 50


Bennet'd often wished before this period of his life that,
instead of spending his whole income, he'd laid by an annual sum for
t'better provision of his children, of his wife, if she survived
him. He now wished it more than ever. Had he done his duty in that
respect, Lydia needn't have been indebted t'her uncle for whatever
of honour/credit could now be purchased for her. T'satisfaction of
prevailing on one of t'most worthless young men in Great Britain t'be
her husband might then have rested in its proper place.

He was seriously concerned that a cause of so little advantage t'anyone
should be forwarded at t'sole expense of his brother-in-law, he
was determined, if possible, t'find out t'extent of his assistance,
and t'discharge t'obligation as soon as he could.

When first Bennet'd married, economy was held t'be perfectly
useless, for, of course, they were t'have a son. T'son was t'join
in cutting off t'entail, as soon as he should be of age, t'widow
and younger children would by that means be provided for. Five daughters

Bennet, for many years after Lydia’s birth,'d been certain that he

Five thousand pounds was settled by marriage articles on Bennet and
t'children. But in what proportions it should be divided amongst the

That it'd be done with such trifling exertion on his side, too, was

T'good news spread quickly through t'house, with proportionate

'twas a fortnight since Bennet'd been downstairs; but on this

“Haye Park might do if t'Gouldings could quit it--or the

Her husband allowed her t'talk on without interruption while the

Bennet, before you take any/all of these houses for your son and

A long dispute followed this declaration; but Bennet was firm. It

Elizabeth was now most heartily sorry that she'd, from t'distress of

She'd no fear of its spreading farther through his means. There were

From such a connection she couldn't wonder that he'd shrink. The

What a triumph for him, as she often thought, could he know that the

She began now t'comprehend that he was exactly t'man who, in

But no such happy marriage could now teach t'admiring multitude what

How Wickham, Lydia were t'be supported in tolerable independence,

Gardiner soon wrote again t'his brother. T'Bennet’s

“'twas greatly my wish that he should do so as soon as

Bennet, his daughters saw all t'advantages of Wickham’s removal

“She's so fond of Forster it'll be quite shocking

His daughter’s request, for such it might be considered, of being

Chapter 51


Their sister’s wedding day arrived; Jane, Elizabeth felt for her

Their reception from Bennet, t'whom they then turned, wasn't quite

“Only think of its being three months since I went away;

“Oh! certainly we'll

On such encouragement t'ask, Elizabeth was forced t'put it out of her

But t'live in ignorance on such a point was impossible;/at least

“Gracechurch street, Sept. 6.

“MY DEAR NIECE,

in t'world of Elizabeth; though at first she often listened with
an astonishment bordering on alarm at her lively, sportive, manner of
talking t'her brother. He, who'd always inspired in herself a respect
which almost overcame her affection, she now saw t'object of open
pleasantry. Her mind received knowledge which'd never fallen
in her way. By Elizabeth’s instructions, she began t'comprehend that
a woman may take liberties with her husband which a brother won't
always allow in a sister more than ten years younger than himself.

Catherine was extremely indignant on t'marriage of her nephew;
and as she gave way t'all t'genuine frankness of her character in
her reply t't'letter which announced its arrangement, she sent him
language so abusive, especially of Elizabeth, that for some time
all intercourse was at an end. But at length, by Elizabeth’s persuasion,
he was prevailed on t'overlook t'offence, seek a reconciliation;
and, after a little further resistance on t'part of his aunt, her
resentment gave way, either t'her affection for him,/her curiosity
t'see how his wife conducted herself; she condescended t'wait
on them at Pemberley, in spite of that pollution which its woods had
received, not merely from t'presence of such a mistress, but the
visits of her uncle, aunt from t'city.

With t'Gardiners, they were always on t'most intimate terms.
Darcy, as well as Elizabeth, really loved them; they were both ever
sensible of t'warmest gratitude towards t'persons who, by bringing
her int'Derbyshire,'d been t'means of uniting them.
